Approval
This thesis has been prepared over seven months at the Department of Civil Engineering,
at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial fulfilment for the degree Master
of Science in Engineering, MSc Eng. The project accounts for 35 ECTS, and has been
carried out between 25/01-21 and 27/08-21. It is assumed that the reader has basic
knowledge within signal processing.

ii
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This thesis seeks to employ an advanced technique for assessing and detecting structural
failures commonly induced to structures exposed to excessive loads, including earthquake
ground motions or wave-induced forces. For this purpose, a numerical investigation is
conducted on the applicability of the Hilbert-Huang transform (HHT) for identifying structural
damage caused by a sudden change of stiffness.
A literature review on the state-of-the-art damage detection research is presented with the
various applications of the HHT for damage detection. From this, the HHT seems to be
an interesting and promising tool for damage detection in civil structures. However, the
method does face some shortcomings; thus, the thesis focuses on presenting some of
them and discussing possible ways of treating them.
For performing damage detection on level 1, i.e. identification of damage existence, four
approaches are chosen to be implemented and further investigate the applicability. The
first two methods are focused on detecting the damage time instant, and the last two are
used to identify natural frequencies before and after damage.
Lastly, the different approaches are tested on three 2D frame structures to perform a sensi-
tivity analysis of the method for detecting damage. The sensitivity parameters investigated
are; frequency content of structure, damage severity, damage location, duration of signals,
noise and bandwidths.
From the results presented in this thesis, the chosen application of the HHT for damage
detection accomplished the desired damage detection to a quite satisfactory degree. The
method’s main advantage is that it can display the frequency-time relations, which is very
useful for nonlinear or non-stationary signals. However, for this thesis, the method was
only applied to linear signals.

Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:17==
1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
Civil structures in general, whether its bridges, wind turbines, buildings, or offshore struc-
tures, can potentially be exposed to excessive loads and are therefore vulnerable to
damage and deterioration during their service life. This can include damage from fatigue,
ageing of materials, corrosion, long-term effect of loads and nature or accidental loads.
Thus, determining the current state and damage of a structure is important for guaranteeing
the reliability of the structure for the safety of the public. This has motivated researchers
and engineers to develop methods that help monitor structures and identify structural
parameters and damage at the earliest possible stage. This is classified as the primary
objective of a structural health monitoring system (SHM), and damage detection is a major
part of this system.
Visual inspections traditionally assess the condition of a structure. However, these methods
are now more likely to become outdated since they can be inefficient and expensive. There-
fore, new methods called Non-Destructive Evaluation Techniques (NDE) are designed.
Traditional NDE methods like acoustic, ultrasonic, radiography methods usually require
that the proximity of the damage is known beforehand and can easily be accessed [1].
To effectively assess the state of a larger or complex structure, vibration-based damage
detection methods have been developed. Since sensors and data acquisition systems
have become more affordable during the previous decades, the use of vibration-based
data to identify structural damage has gained more attention. In contrast to the traditional
NDE methods, the vibration-based methods are based on global vibration response mea-
surements. The basic idea is that changes in the structure’s physical properties, such as
mass, stiffness and damping, are reflected in the modal dynamic properties (frequencies,
mode shapes and modal damping) and thereby the vibration response data. By analyzing
this response utilizing a damage detection algorithm, the damage present in the structure
can be identified.
Many of these new vibration-based damage detection methods are in theory only applicable
to linear signals and usually only consider either the time or frequency domain. However,
since most of the world’s signals are nonlinear and non-stationary, further research and
new, refined methods are needed. To solve these problems, joint time-frequency methods
are designed and can therefore be used on non-stationary signals to evaluate the changes
in frequency over time.
The motivation for this project is to investigate a possible method for damage detection in
structures, which may contribute to the research field of structural health monitoring. It is
therefore chosen in this project to investigate the use of the Hilbert-Huang transform for
damage detection purposes.
1.2
Main Focus and Objective of Project
This project focuses only on structural damage detection, and damage in non-structural
elements has not been evaluated.
Damage can be induced to a structure by element stiffness changes, such as weld fracture,
column buckling, and brace breakage in structures exposed to harsh environments [2].
Generally, damage can be referred to as undesirable changes, for instance, changes in
Damage Detection of Structures
1
==PAGE:18==
the boundary condition, material properties or loosening of connections [3]. Damage can
also be induced to a structure by fatigue, corrosion of steel elements, or damage due to
fire or water.
In this project, damage are simulated by a reduction of stiffness in the different elements.
This will impose a change in the dynamic properties of the structure and is therefore
reflected in the response measurements. By investigating these simulated responses, the
damage can be identified.
The main objective for damage detection schemes in SHM systems is to (1) identify the
existence of damage, (2) locate the damage, (3) quantify the damage severity, and (4)
predict the remaining life span of the structure assessed [4]. In this work, the application
of the chosen method is investigated for only the first part of damage detection. The
existence of damage is identified by determining the damage time instants, as well as
identifying the natural frequencies before and after damage.
Vibration-based damage detection methods can be divided into no-model and model-based
methods. The no-model methods are performed directly on the structural response data to
extract characteristic vibration features. The advantages of using these methods are that
modelling errors, and the uncertainties in modal analysis are avoided, and computational
costs can be reduced. [5]
To investigate the current properties of an existing structure, the loads are usually unknown,
and only response data captured from sensors mounted on the structure is available. These
measurements are also very likely to be contaminated with noise. It is therefore important
to include this aspect when investigating new possible methods for damage detection.
With this in mind, the main objective was to find a new output-only method that uses the
measured acceleration responses, is needless of information about the external loads and
investigate its performance for structural damage detection.
The chosen method for investigation, the Hilbert-Huang Transform (HHT), is a direct and
adaptive method proposed by Huang et al. [6] for nonlinear and non-stationary time-series
analysis. For structural damage detection, it is an output-only method that utilizes the
acceleration responses of a structure, and the loads are not required as input. The method
consists of two parts; the empirical mode decomposition (EMD) and the Hilbert spectral
analysis by utilizing the Hilbert transform (HT).
1.3
Outline of Main Chapters
This report follows the structure and progress of the master’s project. The first 5 weeks
were spent on literature review and investigating existing methodologies used in structural
health monitoring research. Secondly, some time was dedicated to investigating the
different elements of the chosen method, the empirical mode decomposition and the
Hilbert spectral analysis. In the research literature, the HHT is utilized in various ways to
perform structural damage detection. Therefore, some time was also dedicated to choosing
and implementing a damage detection scheme for a frame structure using the HHT. To
ensure the method’s performance, the results from [2] were used to verify the accuracy
of the implementation of the method in this project. Thereafter, a sensitivity analysis was
performed to check the robustness of the applied method for structural damage detection.
Lastly, a discussion of the results and the conclusions drawn from that are presented.
Recommendations for further work associated with this project are given at the end.

Literature review

description of the main focus in the various papers is also provided. The motivation for
investigating the use of the Hilbert Huang transform for damage detection is also further
explained in this chapter.
Methodology and tools
Firstly, the equation of motion for a linear dynamic system is presented, showing how the
reduction of stiffness affects the natural frequencies. Then, the first part of the Hilbert-
Huang transform, the empirical mode decomposition (EMD), is presented, and the different
components of the method are explained in detail. Moreover, the Hilbert spectral analysis
is also introduced, and the parts necessary for damage detection are described. Lastly,
the software and functions utilized in this project are presented.
Finite element model setup

of the implementation.

chosen parameters to investigate are frequency content of structure, damage severity,
damage location, duration of signals, noise, and bandwidths.

for further work.

2
Literature Review
In this chapter, a review of the relevant literature of the topic on damage detection will
be summarised and presented. This was carried out over the first 5 weeks of the thesis
preparation. The objective was to get familiar with structural health monitoring and damage
detection. Moreover, the main focus of the literature review was to explore new vibration-
based methods for damage detection in nonlinear structures and choose one suited for
further investigation.
2.1
Vibration-based Damage Detection Methods
Damage is defined as a change in a structure’s geometric or material characteristics that
unfavourably affect its performance and reliability [7]. This indicates that damage are not
necessarily regarded as a complete failure of a system but a degradation of the structure’s
capability to perform optimally. Damage identification can be performed at 4 levels, as
defined by Rytter [8]:
• Level 1: Identification of damage existence
• Level 2: Identification of damage existence and localization of the damage
• Level 3: Identification of damage existence, localization of damage and quantification
of the damage severity
• Level 4: Identification of damage existence, localization of damage, quantification of
the damage severity and prediction of the remaining life span of the structure.
Damage can be classified to have linear or nonlinear effects on the structure. For example,
when an initially linear-elastic structure remains in the linear-elastic regime, the damage
is linear. In this case, the structural response can be modelled using linear equations of
motion, where the changes in the modal properties result from geometry or material property
changes. On the other hand, if the structure behaves in a nonlinear manner after damage,
it is considered a nonlinear damage type. This can be, for instance, fatigue, breathing
cracks, loosening of connections or nonlinear material behaviour due to excessive loading.
The damaged structures can potentially endanger the public’s safety and lead to economic
losses if not detected prior to failures. Developing methods for detecting damage at the
earliest possible stage has therefore been an important topic for decades [9]. As a result,
non-destructive damage detection methods have been designed to detect damage without
imposing more damage to the structure. These can include methods as visual inspection,
ultrasonic, X-ray and acoustic emissions [1]. However, these methods are local and are
limited to damage that is known a priori and that the damaged part of the structure is easily
accessible. Therefore, this has led to the need for global methods for damage detection
of larger and complex structures, and vibration-based methods were developed. In this
review, the focus is directed to the global damage detection methods only.
The basic idea behind using vibration data to detect damage is that the modal parameters
as natural frequencies, mode shapes and modal damping are functions of the physical pa-
rameters; mass, damping and stiffness. Furthermore, changes in the physical parameters
are reflected in the modal response data that can be captured by measuring the vibrations
of a structure. Global vibration-based damage detection methods can be divided into two
categories; model-based and response-based methods [9].
Damage Detection of Structures
5
==PAGE:22==
It is assumed that the structure responds in a predefined manner for the model-based
methods, which is usually simulated using a finite element model. The damage location
and severity can then be identified by comparing the updated model, which is correlated
with the measured data of the damaged structure, to the original model. In most cases,
the model-based methods are used for detecting damage on Levels 2 and 3. For example,
Jaishi et al. [10]. proposed a sensitivity based FE-model updating method for damage
detection in a beam using modal flexibility residual. This was verified both with numerical
and experimental tests. However, the model-based methods in general also have some
limitations regarding modal identification, which is inevitable.
The response-based methods are either directly or indirectly applied to the structural
response, and the changes in the modal parameters between the intact and damaged
state are utilized to identify damage in the structure. An example of this is the proposed
method by Ditommaso et al. [11], where the Stockwell-transform is utilized to follow the
modal curvature variation for damage detection purposes in frame structures under seismic
excitation.
Global vibration damage detection methods can further be divided into input-output and
output-only methods. The input-output methods use a known input loading and the
measured responses of the structure to compare the undamaged and potentially damaged
structure to identify damage. For example, Prawin and Rao [12] proposed a nonlinear
parametric identification algorithm with a describing function technique combined with
a principal component analysis model (PCA-model). The non-parametric PCA-model
has the ability to predict the response for any input loading; hence it is regarded and an
input-output method. Another input-output method proposed by Moaveni and Asgarieh
[13] uses the deterministic-stochastic subspace identification (DSI) method to perform
instantaneous modal analysis of nonlinear dynamic systems and comparing the results
with those obtained from the Wavelet transform (WT).
The output-only methods can perform with only structural responses measured on the
structure, where the input excitations are unknown. Methods like the ones proposed in [5]
and [14] have the advantage of only being dependent on the vibration response, which
does not require suspension of the normal operation of the structure. Ebrahimian et al.
[14] used nonlinear FE-model updating in the time-domain for damage identification based
on the Bayesian inference to simultaneously estimate unknown model parameters and the
ground motion time series. In comparison, Bao et al. [5] proposed an improved Hilbert-
Huang transform method for system identification and damage detection in structures
under ambient excitations. The method presented is a multi-stage damage detection
scheme only relying upon the structural acceleration responses. Both damage time-instant
and damage locations were identified in this study, together with qualitatively assessing
the damage severity.
Vibration-based damage detection methods are performed either in the time, frequency,
or joint time-frequency domain. The methods in [14] and [15] are both time-domain
methods based on the extended Kalman filter. These types of damage detection methods
eliminate errors associated with frequency transformations, such as leakage and truncation.
However, in some cases the volume of data analyzed can be greatly reduced by utilizing a
frequency-domain method, which in turn can greatly reduce the computational time. In both
[16] and [17], the frequency response function is used for performing damage detection
in the frequency domain. However, in real life most data is nonlinear or non-stationary,
so joint time-frequency methods have been developed to for instance evaluate changes
in frequency over time. Examples of these methods can be the Hilbert-Huang transform
6
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:23==
shown in [5] for damage detection in frame structures or the Wavelet transform used
in [18] for damage detection of a mixed concrete and steel frame subjected to seismic
excitations. In addition, Ditommaso et al. [11] used the Stockwell transform, which is also
in the time-frequency domain, to detect damage by analyzing the modal curvature.
2.2
Hilbert-Huang Transform for Damage Detection
Physical or numerical data most likely have one of these problems: (1) too short data, (2)
non-stationary data, and (3) data representing a nonlinear process. In these cases, there
are limited options to use for analyzing the data. Huang et al. [6], therefore, proposed
the Hilbert-Huang Transform as a new method to process nonlinear and non-stationary
signals. The method combines the well-known Hilbert spectral analysis (HSA) and the
newly developed empirical mode decomposition (EMD). By combining the EMD with the
HSA, Huang et al. [6] showed that the method obtained good results for various data,
such as classical nonlinear equations and natural phenomena. Unfortunately, the EMD
algorithms and HSA methods have been patented by NASA, limiting the application of the
method in the scientific community.
The method has been widely applied in geophysics, such as climate variability [19], nonlin-
ear ocean waves [20] and orbital drift artifacts in satellite data stream [21]. The Hilbert-
Huang Transform has also been applied in structural engineering, especially for SHM. As
shown in [22], [23], [24], [25], [26], [27], and [28], the HHT is used for damage detection
in civil structures, both for linear and nonlinear cases. The method is output-only, hence
it has the advantages of only depending on the acceleration response measurements
and no suspension of the daily operation and use of the structures is necessary. The
HHT is also applicable to nonlinear and non-stationary signals, as described in [6]. From
these statements, the HHT appears to be an interesting and promising tool for structural
damage detection. Therefore, literature on the HHT for damage detection purposes has
been reviewed further.
Chiang et al. [22] utilized the HHT for detecting sensitivity of damage by using the ratio of
rotation (RR), the ratio of shifting value (SV) and the ratio of bandwidth (RB) as damage
indices. Three types of multiple degree-of-freedom (MDOF) structures were analyzed
numerically with seismic excitations. The authors concluded that the RB obtained from the
Hilbert spectrum was found to have an incremental change with increasing RR, making
this damage index sensitive for detecting structural damage.
Experimental studies have also been conducted using the HHT for damage detection
purposes. Zhou and Yan [23] performed an experimental study using the HHT for linear
system identification and damage detection. To simulate damage, columns at different
locations were removed. The structure was excited by an impulse load at the top of the
structure, while sensors placed at each floor recorded the responses. A linear least-squares
fit procedure was used to fit the logarithm of amplitudes, and phase angles, to estimate the
damping ratios and natural frequencies. Kunwar et al. [24] demonstrated the use of the HHT
for damage detection and localization of damage in a single-span bridge with 10 sensors
under transient vibrations. Different levels of damage were introduced by removing bolts
in one girder. In this study, the damage were detected by comparing the baseline Hilbert
spectrum and phase with the ones from the damaged state. Vibration magnitude, peak
frequency in the Hilbert marginal spectrum and phase angle were compared for different
sensor locations to locate the damage. Xu and Chen [28] performed an experimental study
on a frame structure using the EMD to detect a sudden stiffness reduction by releasing
springs attached to the test structure. The experiment was conducted with 28 test cases,
including free vibrations, white noise excitations and earthquake ground motions.
Damage Detection of Structures
7
==PAGE:24==
Some studies also combined the HHT with a machine learning approach to detect damage.
Vazirizade et al. [27] proposed a damage detection scheme using the HHT and artificial
neural networks (ANN). An extended method of the EMD is applied in this study, the
ensemble empirical mode decomposition (EEMD), for identifying the first mode frequency
and mode shape. Thereafter, the ANN is utilized to predict story acceleration based on
previous values measured. Mousavi et al. [29] combined the complete ensemble empirical
mode decomposition with adaptive noise (CEEMDAN) and ANN to detect damage in a
steel truss bridge. By comparing four key features obtained from the CEEMDAN-HHT of
the intact and damaged case, the damage’s presence, severity, and location are assessed.
The Hilbert-Huang transform has also been applied to detect damage during seismic
events. For instance, Bahar and Ramezani [26] proposed an enhanced HHT for detecting
damage in the Van Nuys Hotel building under the Northridge earthquake. The damage
were identified by tracking the phase and frequency properties obtained from different
sensor locations. Wang et al. [25] used the HHT to identify the instantaneous frequency
and viscous damping coefficient of a recycled aggregate concrete frame structure under
seismic loading. The proposed approach is an input-output method focusing on estimating
the nonlinear story force.
For determining natural frequencies before and after damage, Yang et al. [2] proposed
a method based on computing the frequency-time relation of each modal component
associated with the largest amplitude in the Hilbert spectrum. The authors also propose
two methods of determining the damage time instant; one using the EMD for extracting
damage spikes due to a sudden reduction of stiffness, and another by tracking the frequency
over time. This application of the HHT for level 1 damage detection has been investigated
further in this project. In addition to that, a similar method as proposed in [30] has been
followed in this project to identify natural frequencies before and after damage by using
HHT and a linear least-square fit on the instantaneous phase angles.
The Fourier transform, a popular tool among current signal-processing methods, has high
resolution in the frequency domain but no resolution in the time domain. On the other
hand, the Wavelet transform has a high resolution in both the time and frequency domain,
though with some limitations regarding false harmonics that can further disturb the analysis
[31]. The studies presented in [32], [33], [6] show that the HHT perform even better than
the Wavelet for identifying nonlinear systems. In addition to that, the resolution of the
Hilbert spectrum based on the EMD is shown to be higher than the one from the Wavelet
spectrum, both in time and frequency.
From the literature review done in this work, the HHT seems to be a good method for
analyzing nonlinear and non-stationary signals and for damage detection in civil structures.
It also appears to yield better results compared to other time-frequency methods. However,
the literature also shed light on the many challenges and difficulties that concern the HHT
based damage detection techniques for structural engineering. Some of these will be
addressed in the next chapter.
8
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:25==
3
Methodology and Tools
In this section, the method of the Hilbert-Huang transform introduced in chapter 2 will be
discussed further and explained in detail. Since the chosen method of the empirical mode
decomposition was unknown to the project´s supervisors, a lot of work was dedicated to
investigate and understand the method. Moreover, the HHT is also applied in various
ways for civil engineering purposes, which makes it even more challenging.
3.1
Equation of Motion of Dynamic System for Damage
Detection
For an intact, linear dynamic system with no damage present, the equation of motion is
written as
[M0]{¨x(t)} + [C0]{ ˙x(t)} + [K0]{x(t)} = {f(t)}
(3.1)
where [M0], [C0] and [K0] are the mass, damping and stiffness matrices respectively; {f(t)}
is the external load vector; and {¨x(t)}, { ˙x(t)}, {x(t)} are correspondingly the acceleration,
velocity and displacement response vectors.
It is assumed that the modes are characterized as normal, and therefore the multi-degree-
of-freedom (MDOF) system can be decoupled into modal components. The modal charac-
teristics as natural frequencies can be identified for each natural mode. These parameters
are the fundamental characteristic of a structural system and are defined by the physical
properties of the structure. Hence, the modal parameters can be further investigated for
damage detection.
When structural damage occurs, it will be represented in the discretised system as changes
to [M0], [C0] and [K0]. In general, no changes in mass are assumed to occur before and
after damage. Furthermore, damage in structures normally lead to a decrease in structural
stiffness as well as an increase in structural damping. However, since the majority of
structural damage is caused by a reduction of stiffness, only this type of damage is
considered in this project.
If a structure is subjected to an extreme event and some elements become damaged,
the stiffness, [K0], is assumed to change to [Kd], see Equation 3.2. The change in
stiffness, [∆K], will also result in a change in the natural frequencies as given as shown in
Equation 3.3.
[∆K] = [K0] −[Kd]
(3.2)
{∆ωj} = {ω0
j } −{ωd
j }.
(3.3)
3.2
Empirical Mode Decomposition
The first part of the chosen methodology, HHT, is the empirical mode decomposition.
It is defined as an adaptive and direct method, and therefore an efficient algorithm to
decompose any data set into a finite number of ’Intrinsic Mode Functions’ (IMFs). The
EMD is based on the local characteristic time scale of the data; hence it is suitable for
nonlinear and non-stationary signals.[6]
Damage Detection of Structures
9
==PAGE:26==
3.2.1
Intrinsic Mode Functions
The term intrinsic mode function is adopted since it is defined to represent an oscillation
mode embedded in the original signal that is decomposed. To define a component as an
intrinsic mode function, it must satisfy two conditions:
1. the number of extrema and the number of zero-crossings, within the whole data set,
should be equal or differ by at most one
2. the mean value of the envelope defined by the local minima and the envelope defined
by the local maxima is zero at any point. [6]
With this definition, the IMFs are not restricted to be narrow-banded signals, and they can
also be both time- and frequency-modulated. See Figure 3.1 for a typical IMF.
0
5
10
15
20
25
30
Time, t [s]
-0.05
0
0.05
[m/s2]
IMF
Figure 3.1: Example of a typical IMF
The first condition ensures that no complex riding waves are included in the IMFs. This
means that waves with positive local minima or negative local maxima are eliminated from
the functions decomposed by the EMD. The second condition is included to avoid unwanted
fluctuations in the instantaneous frequency (see subsection 3.3.3) due to asymmetric
waveforms. Ideally, the second requirement would state that the ’local mean’ should be
zero. However, for non-stationary signals, a ’local time scale’ cannot be defined. Thus,
the local mean is approximated from the envelopes defined by the local maxima and local
minima.
3.2.2
The sifting process
The EMD algorithm processes any signal into several IMFs. This procedure can also be
referred to as a sifting process. It is a direct and adaptive method, where the decomposition
is based and derived from the data. The method identifies the oscillatory modes by
their characteristic time scales in the data empirically and then decomposes the data
correspondingly.
The decomposition is based on these assumptions:
1. the data has at least two extrema, i.e. one maximum and one minimum
2. the characteristic time scale is defined by the time interval between extrema
3. if the signal has no extrema, the signal can be differentiated once or several times to
reveal the extrema. In this case, the final results can be obtained by integration(s) of
the different components obtained. [6]
The time-lapse between two successive extrema is utilized instead of two zero-crossings to
define the characteristic time scale for the oscillatory modes. In this way, the decomposition
can be applied to data with non-zero mean and without zero-crossings.
Following the definition of an IMF, the mean value of the signal is calculated from the
envelopes defined from the maxima and minima respectively. Thus, the first step in the
10
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:27==
sifting process is to locate all extrema to define the envelopes by cubic spline interpolation.
The mean between these two envelopes, designated as m1, is then subtracted from
the original signal, X(t). This gives the first component, h1, of the sifting process, see
Equation 3.4. This step is illustrated in Figure 3.2.
X(t) −m1 = h1
(3.4)

The first component, h1, shown in Figure 3.2c, is still not an IMF, as it does not fulfil
the symmetry criteria related to the local mean being zero. Ideally, it should be an IMF.
However, overshoots and undershoots in signals are common and can potentially be
generated into new extrema through the sifting process, as shown in Figure 3.2 between
4s and 5s. Although the effect is not direct since the mean is used in the sifting process
and not the envelopes, this problem is real. Another issue is that the envelope mean may
be different from the actual local mean for nonlinear data, meaning that some asymmetric
waveform may still exist even though the data has been sifted several times [6]. This
approximation is accepted.
To eliminate riding waves and asymmetric wave profiles, the sifting procedure needs to
be repeated more times. In this second step, h1, is treated as the data, and the same
procedure computing the envelopes as illustrated in Figure 3.2 is repeated so that

applied with caution regarding this issue, to avoid over-sifting. To stop the sifting process,
the standard deviation computed from two consecutive sifting results is defined as

r1 −c2 = r2, . . . , rn−1 −cn = rn.
(3.10)
The EMD algorithm can be stopped when; either the component, cn, or the residue, rn,
is smaller than a predefined value of substantial consequence, or if the residue, rn, is a
monotonic function and no more IMF can be extracted from. The residue will represent
the mean trend of the signal if it is nonzero-mean. However, if the signal is zero-mean, the
final residue may still be different from zero. The EMD algorithm is illustrated in Figure 3.3.
3.2.3
Cauchy type stop criteria
To get meaningful IMFs, the number of iterations for each IMF should be kept low to avoid
constant amplitudes in the IMF by over-sifting. As shown in [34], if the number of siftings is
very high, the IMF component extracted becomes a pure frequency-modulated signal with
constant amplitude. This occurs due to the fact that the natural cubic spline interpolation
of the envelope tends to result in a straight line when extrema are sparsely distributed
compared to the sampling data.
12
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:29==
Figure 3.3: Flow chart of EMD algorithm
The criterion for stopping the sifting process from extracting IMFs is set to be when the
standard deviation between two consecutive sifting results is less than a value between
0.2 and 0.3, as shown in Equation 3.8. This criterion is defined with the global property
since it is integrated over the whole signal domain. It is also heavily influenced by small
values in the hk-components at certain locations.
Another variation of the Cauchy type criterion proposed by [34] should be more stable than
the previous one. This is defined as
SD =

These two different stopping criteria showed to yield almost identical results for the specific
applications in this project. Therefore, it was chosen to apply the first criterion presented
for further analyses.

Furthermore, the maximum number of IMFs is chosen to be ’10’ so that the decomposition
of IMFs stops when the chosen number of IMFs is reached. This is implemented to save
computational time since the results in this work were not affected by only including the
first 10 IMFs.
3.2.4
Boundary effect
The boundary effect occurring at the beginning and end of a signal is another well-known
shortcoming of the method. This problem stems from the definition of the envelopes using
the cubic splines, where large swings at the ends can occur. If left unattended, the end
fluctuations can potentially propagate inward and corrupt the whole signal component
extracted. This issue typically occurs in the lower frequency components during the sifting
process.
Axis symmetry signal extension
The most common procedure recommended for this problem is signal extension methods.
Huang et al. [6] proposed a method by adding two characteristic waves at the beginning
and end of the signal based on the frequency and amplitude of the consecutive ones.
However, since NASA protects the method as a US patent, people fail to follow their
method. Instead, the authors of [35] proposed a method extending the signal by mirroring
extrema, called axis-symmetry signal extension (ASSE). The process of this method is
also described in [35]. By utilizing the EMD with ASSE, the large swings appearing due
to the boundary effect from the spline interpolation are shown to be reduced compared
to the envelopes obtained from the standard EMD algorithm, see Figure 3.4. Whereas,
Figure 3.5 shows how the EMD with ASSE decomposes the multi-component signal into
correct frequency components, i.e. the IMFs, compared to the decomposition obtained
from the standard EMD approach. The multi-component signal consists of two cosines
and one sine wave with different frequencies. It can be seen from Figure 3.5a that the
classical EMD method cannot separate and decompose the lowest frequency component
of the signal. In comparison, Figure 3.5b shows all three components decomposed into
IMF1, IMF2 and IMF3 by using the EMD with ASSE.
Cosine window
The cosine window approach for controlling the boundary distortion from the EMD was
proposed by Qi et al. [36]. Its efficiency to decompose signals correctly was compared
to a signal extension method and an improved stopping criterion. The cosine window is
defined as

When applying the cosine window to the same multi-component signal, the IMFs obtained
are shown in Figure 3.5c. Thus, the first three components of the signal can be identified
using this approach. However, since the signal in this example is relatively short, the
amplitudes in the lowest frequency component, IMF3, are affected by the windowing
compared to IMF3 obtained from using EMD with ASSE.
It is chosen to utilize the signal extension method further in this work to treat the EMD
algorithm’s boundary problem. This is because the signal extension method is widely used
in the literature compared to the cosine window. The window is not recommended for short
data signals since it removes potentially important information in the signal. On the other
hand, the signal extension method is also not perfect for treating the boundary problem.
The authors of [36] recommended a combination of the cosine window and the signal

Furthermore, only stationary signals are included in the analyses of the method, so it is
not necessary to include both methods for treating boundary distortion.
3.2.5
Bandpass filtering
Both, due to the issue of boundary distortion and mode mixing problems in civil engineering
purposes, the EMD may not be able to decompose the signal correctly. This can for instance
be that one IMF may cover more than one modal frequency component or that the EMD
produces pseudo components. Therefore, a pre-processing scheme is employed to filter
the wideband signal into several narrow-banded sub-signals to solve these problems [5].
To obtain the different modal responses, band pass filtering is applied to the measured
acceleration responses, ¨xp(t), where p = 1, 2, ...n is the sensor number. From the Fourier
spectrum identified from the structural response measurements, approximate frequency
ranges for each natural frequency, ωj, can be determined, i.e. ωjL < ωj < ωjH(j =
1, 2, ...n).
The resulting narrow-banded sub-signal obtained is processed through the EMD, and
the first IMF extracted is relatively close to the jth modal response [30]. Thus, the modal
responses are easily obtained by following this approach, and the noises outside the
frequency range are removed.

3.3
Hilbert Spectral Analysis
The second part of the chosen methodology is the Hilbert spectral analysis (HSA). A new
way of considering a random signal X(t) was suggested in the 1940s to be a product of
two independent functions, A(t) and cos(θ(t)) such that
X(t) = A(t) cos(θ(t)).
(3.13)
This way, the signal X(t) can represent a harmonic fluctuation modulated both in amplitude
and phase. Furthermore, it is possible to associate any real signal, X(t), with an infinite
number of pairs [A(t), θ(t)]. For such pairs, there should be a one-to-one correspondence
with the real signal, X(t), to define it as in Equation 3.13. To solve this problem, the real
signal X(t) is associated with its analytical signal, Z(t), see Equation 3.14. If X(t) is a real
16
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:33==
signal, then the real part of the analytical signal Z(t) will be equal to X(t); hence there is
a one-to-one correspondence between X(t) and Z(t). In conclusion, the analytical signal
makes it possible to define a unique pair [A(t), θ(t)] with any real signal.
Z(t) = X(t) + iY (t) = A(t)eiθ(t)
(3.14)
The imaginary part of the analytical signal, Y (t), is denominated as the Hilbert transform
(HT) of X(t). The Hilbert transform is an integral transform, similar to the Fourier, named
after David Hilbert, who used it to solve a special case of integral equations. It is defined
as follows;
H[X(t)] = Y (t) = π−1
Z ∞
−∞
X(τ)
t −τ dτ.
(3.15)
This integral is a convolution of X(t) with (πt)−1. However, for discrete-time signals,
the integral form of obtaining the Hilbert transform is not utilized. Instead, the analytical
signal is composed by taking the Fourier transform of the real-valued signal, X(t), and
replacing the Fourier coefficients corresponding to negative frequencies to zero, as shown
in Equation 3.16.
Z[m] =








for m = 0

for 1 ≤m ≤N

for N
2 + 1 ≤m ≤N −1
(3.16)
This way, the Fourier spectra will be one-sided, which will result in a complex-valued signal
in the time domain, i.e. Z(t). The analytical signal is obtained by taking the ifft (inverse
fast Fourier transform) of Z[m].
Thereafter, the HT is determined as the imaginary part, Y (t), of the analytical signal,
Z(t). In another way, the Hilbert transform, Y (t), is related to the real-valued signal, X(t),
by a 90-degree phase shift without changing the amplitudes or the spectral values, see
Figure 3.7.

3.3.1
Instantaneous amplitude
The reason for transforming the real-valued signal, X(t), into the complex plane is to be
able to define the concepts of instantaneous amplitude, phase and frequency. At any time
instant, a vector can be drawn from the t-axis in a perpendicular plane to intersect the
helical complex signal, Z(t), see Figure 3.8
Damage Detection of Structures
17
==PAGE:34==
Figure 3.8: Instantaneous amplitude illustrated with the real and imaginary part of the
analytical signal
The instantaneous amplitude, A(t), is defined as the amplitude of the envelope of the
signal, as shown in Equation 3.17.
A(t) =
p
X2(t) + Y 2(t)
(3.17)
The instantaneous amplitude, A(t), differs from the amplitude of the actual signal as it is
calculated from both the real and imaginary part of the analytical signal, Z(t). This is why
the instantaneous amplitude may not have its maxima at the same time as the real signal,
X(t). Figure 3.9 illustrates how the amplitude, A(t), of the analytical signal is slightly
different from the amplitude of the real signal, X(t), since it also takes the amplitude of
the imaginary part into account.

The envelope function, i.e. the instantaneous amplitude A(t), contains important informa-

3.3.2
Instantaneous phase
The angle, which is related to the orientation of the amplitude vector in Figure 3.8 at time t
is defined as the phase of the analytical signal Z(t). The phase angle can be calculated at
18

any time instant; hence it is called the instantaneous phase, θ(t).

determined as:

3.3.3
Instantaneous frequency
In order to understand nonlinear and non-stationary processes, the instantaneous fre-
quency (IF) is important to investigate. In the literature, the instantaneous frequency is
widely defined as the time-differentiated phase angle, see Equation 3.19.
ω(t) = dθ(t)
dt
= 1
A2
 xy′ −yx′
(3.19)
This is the angular frequency in radians per second. However, if the instantaneous
frequency should be computed in hertz in discrete time, the following formula is utilized;
f(t) = ω(t) fs
2π,
(3.20)
where fs = 1/dt is the sampling frequency.
Due to the discontinuities in the wrapped phase function, the unwrapped phase is utilized
to compute the instantaneous frequency. Using the definition for determining the instanta-
neous frequency given in Equation 3.19, there is a single value defined for each time step
of the signal. Hence, it can only represent one frequency component and therefore it is
important that the signal analyzed, X(t), is ’monocomponent’. The original signal which
may contain more than one frequency at a time locally should therefore be decomposed
into intrinsic mode functions with the EMD prior to the Hilbert spectral analysis.
Damage Detection of Structures
19
==PAGE:36==
The importance of using the intrinsic mode functions for defining the instantaneous fre-
quencies was demonstrated in [6] with a simple function;
x(t) = a + cos(αt),
(3.21)
where a is an arbitrary constant, and α is the wave frequency. The signal’s Hilbert transform
is then simply
y(t) = sin(αt),
(3.22)
and therefore the instantaneous frequency from Equation 3.19 can be written as:
ω(t) =
α(1 + a sin(αt))
1 + 2a cos(αt) + a2 .
(3.23)
To recover the actual frequency, α, of the input signal, x(t), the constant a must be zero.
Hence, for the instantaneous frequency with the analytical signal approach to have physical
meaning, the real signal investigated x(t) has to be ’monocomponent’, locally zero mean,
and the waveforms need to be symmetric about the zero mean. From the definition of the
intrinsic mode functions given in section 3.2.1, the IMFs satisfies all of these conditions.
This again demonstrated the importance of decomposing the signal into IMFs to compute
physically meaningful instantaneous frequencies.
Figure 3.11b shows an example of the instantaneous frequency for a multi-component
signal computed using the HHT. This illustrates how the method is able to separate the
three different frequency components in the signal and determine the evolution of the
frequency over time. It can be seen from Figure 3.11b that the instantaneous frequency
has some fluctuations. These occur due to intrawave frequency modulations from slight
amplitude variations in the IMFs. According to [6], the intrawave frequency modulation
occurring from amplitude variations should be small compared to nonlinear effects.

There have been various criticisms against using the analytical signal for defining the
instantaneous frequency. For instance, the definition of the instantaneous frequency
20
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:37==
given in Equation 3.19 makes it possible to obtain negative frequencies depending on the
slope of the instantaneous phase, which has no physical meaning. Negative frequencies
occur when multi-extrema occur between two zero-crossings, causing the local loops in
the complex plane not to be centred around the origin [37], see an example of this in
Figure 3.12 between 3s and 4s. Another reason why negative frequencies might occur is
large amplitude fluctuations, which also may cause the loops to miss the origin.

IF
Figure 3.12: Example of signal leading to negative instantaneous frequency
To avoid this problem, Huang et al. [37] proposed some different methods to compute the
instantaneous frequency, one of them involving the normalized Hilbert Transform (NHT).
This approach is based on separating the IMFs empirically into envelope (AM) and carrier
(FM) parts. In this way, the instantaneous frequency can be calculated from the purely
frequency modulated signal and avoid fluctuations due to amplitude variations.
The FM part of the signal is obtained by an iterative normalization process. For a given
IMF, the extrema are firstly located, and the envelope, e1(t), is computed using a cubic
spline curve. The IMF, x(t), is then normalized as;
y1(t) = x(t)
e1(t).
(3.24)
This procedure is then repeated n times iteratively as;
y2(t) = y1(t)
e2(t),
...
yn(t) = yn−1(t)
en(t) ,
(3.25)
until the data, yn(t), has no amplitudes higher than unity. This is designated as the
empirical FM part of the signal, F(t), where the AM part of the signal is obtained as;
A(t) = x(t)
F(t).
(3.26)
Damage Detection of Structures
21
==PAGE:38==
Figure 3.13 illustrates the different instantaneous frequencies computed using the tradi-
tional HT and the NHT. For the case with the IMF computed using the EMD with ASSE,
the instantaneous frequencies do not have any rapid variations. Conversely, the IMF
produced from the EMD function provided by MATLAB results in some larger spikes in the
IF due to large amplitude variations. This shows again that producing relevant IMFs is very
important for obtaining meaningful instantaneous frequencies. It is also illustrated that
the NHT does not substantially improve the IF compared to the traditional HT in the case
where EMD with ASSE is used. Finally, it should be noted that the EMD algorithm provided
by MATLAB uses a different stopping criterion for the sifting process, which affects the
IMFs it composes.

and NHT for computing IF
When computing the instantaneous frequency with the analytical signal approach, the
signal should mathematically obey the Bedrosian [38] and Nuttall [39] theorems to give
physically meaningful results. The Bedrosian theorem states that the Hilbert transform of
the carrier should be separable from its envelope as
H{a(t) cos θ(t)} = a(t)H{cos θ(t)},
(3.27)
to obtain meaningful analytical signals for computing the instantaneous frequencies. The
EMD does not produce IMFs that automatically satisfies this theorem since they are not
restricted to be narrow-banded. For that reason, the AM variations may contaminate the
FM part if their Fourier spectra overlap. Unless a bandpass filter is used, the Bedrosian
theorem is violated by any local amplitude change [40]. The NHT satisfies the condition in
22
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:39==
the Bedrosian theorem by separating the amplitude and the frequency part of the signal
and computing the instantaneous frequency from the FM part only.
The Nuttall theorem, on the other hand, states that if the instantaneous phase function,
θ(t), is not a narrow band function, H[cos(θ(t))] will not necessarily be sin(θ(t)). This
states that the analytical pairs obtained from the Hilbert transform may not be identical to
the quadrature. Most investigators have ignored this problem using the analytical signal
approach to compute the instantaneous frequency with the HT [37].
The application of the standard HT for computing the instantaneous frequencies is still
utilized in this project, as the results were not affected by improving the HT. This is because
bandpass filtering is applied to the measured responses, and all simulations are considered
linear.

the analytical signal can be expressed as

3.4
Software
The software used for applying the presented methodology is MATLAB, and the main
functions utilized are presented shortly.
3.4.1
EMD functions
There are two different EMD functions presented in this project. The first one is the emd.m
provided by MATLAB. This function uses the stopping criterion defined in Equation 3.11.
However, another EMD function has rather been utilized in this project, which uses the
stopping criterion defined in Equation 3.8 proposed by Huang et al. [6]. It is originally
written by Ivan Magrin-Chagnolleau, see Appendix A.1. However, the script has been
extended in this project to include the axis-symmetry signal extension (ASSE) method,
see Appendix A.2. The function utilized for performing the ASSE can be found in Appendix
A.3, written by G. Rilling [41].
3.4.2
Hilbert spectral analysis functions
For performing the Hilbert spectral analysis, the provided functions in MATLAB’s signal
processing toolbox are utilized. This includes; hilbert.m for computing the analytical signal
and hht.m for calculating the instantaneous frequency and the Hilbert amplitude spectrum.
The hilbert-function can be utilized to determine the real and imaginary part of the complex
signal and thus compute the instantaneous phase and amplitudes as shown in Equa-
tion 3.18 and 3.17. Furthermore, the hht-function uses MATLAB’s gradient function to
differentiate the phase function for calculating the instantaneous frequency numerically.
Lastly, the function for calculating the NTH can be found in Appendix B.1.
3.4.3
Finite element modelling functions
The basis for all finite element modelling is the scripts provided by Jan Becker Høgsberg
in the ’Dynamics of structures: theory and analysis’ course (41237) at DTU. This includes
the scripts timeintframe.m and framevid2D.m to perform Newmark time integration and
modal analysis for 2D structures.
24
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:41==
4
Finite Element Model Setup
This chapter is dedicated to describing the finite element model setup and structural models
used for investigating the application of the HHT for damage detection. The structural
responses are simulated so that the first part of the signal is intact and the second part
is after the structure has been damaged. In this project, damage is only simulated as a
sudden reduction of stiffness. Three structures are utilized for this purpose; a 2D 3-storey
frame (structure A), a 2D 4-storey frame (structure B) and a 2D 2-bay 4-storey frame
(structure C).
4.1
Finite Element Model
The finite element model (FE-model) is described in this section, where the elements
utilized and the damping model are described. In addition, the chosen approach for
simulating the response with damage is also presented in this section.
4.1.1

The beam elements used for modelling the three structures in this project are Euler-
Bernoulli beams, suitable for small deflections, i.e. the linear regime of a structure. The
mass matrix for each element is defined as

The beam element used for modelling is a two-node beam, shown in Figure 4.1. It has 3
degrees of freedom at each node; two translational and one rotational degree of freedom.
4.1.2
Damping model
In this work, damage is simulated only as a stiffness reduction of elements by reducing the
Young’s modulus, E. This will also reduce the natural frequencies; hence, the damping is
affected as it is defined by Rayleigh damping. The damping is chosen to be calibrated
Damage Detection of Structures
25
==PAGE:42==
Figure 4.1: Two node beam element with 6 degrees of freedom
from the first and the third mode to define the Rayleigh damping coefficients, α and β, see
Equation 4.3.
α = 2 ζ

ω1 ω3
(ω1 + ω3)

,
β =
2 ζ
(ω1 + ω3),
(4.3)
where ζ is the damping ratio and ω1 and ω3 are the first and third natural frequency
respectively.
The damping matrix for the intact state is then constructed from the known matrices, [K0]
and [M0], as shown in Equation 4.4.
[C0] = α [M0] + β [K0]
(4.4)
For the damaged state, the damping matrix is defined from the same Rayleigh damp-
ing coefficients as for the intact state, but with the damaged stiffness matrix, [Kd], see
Equation 4.5.
[Cd] = α [M0] + β [Kd]
(4.5)
4.1.3
Response simulation
The response is simulated by using Newmark time integration for linear dynamic systems,
see code in Appendix C.1. This is an implicit integration scheme for calculating displace-
ments, velocities and accelerations in each degree of freedom at each time step. For this
project, dt = 0.001 s is chosen so that the sampling frequency is fs = 1000 Hz, as it also
has been used by other researchers on the same topic, for instance, Lin et al. [42] and
Aied et al. [43].
Simulating damage as reduction of stiffness
The simulation of acceleration responses under a horizontal random excitation, f(t), is
accomplished through two steps. First, the response is simulated with the intact structural
properties, i.e. intact stiffness matrix and damping matrix up until time instant, td. This
is the time instant of damage. Secondly, the end conditions for the displacement vector,
{x(td)}, and velocity vector, { ˙x(td)}, are used as the initial conditions for simulating the
damaged response and using [Kd] and [Cd] as defined in section 4.1.2. The MATLAB
code written for this purpose can be found in Appendix C.2. It is based on timeintframe.m
mentioned in section 3.4.3.
4.1.4
Modal analysis
Modal analysis is performed for both the intact and damaged states of the structure
to calculate the natural frequencies before and after damage. These are then used to
compare with the frequencies estimated using the HHT.
26
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:43==
This is done by solving the eigenvalue problem
 [K] −{ω2
n} [M]

(4.6)
where {U} is the mode shape vector (eigenvector) and {ω2
n} are the eigenvalues. Thus,
the natural frequencies are determined by taking the square root of the eigenvalues.

5
Implementation of the Hilbert-Huang
Transform for Damage Detection
In this chapter, the implementation of the Hilbert-Huang transform is described by showing
an example of each part of the damage detection scheme. The literature discussed in
chapter 2 shows many different ways of performing damage detection by utilizing the
Hilbert-Huang transform. It is chosen to follow the proposed methods in [2] and a similar
procedure to the one in [42]. Both articles investigate the application of the method on the
ASCE Benchmark structure; hence it is chosen to use Structure C (ref. 4.4) to validate the
application of the chosen methodology.
The HHT is implemented for detecting damage on level 1, i.e. detecting the existence of
damage. In addition to that, the damage time instant is also identified. Furthermore, two
different approaches are evaluated and compared to detect the damage time instant and
identify natural frequencies before and after damage.
The structure has four sensors, one in each floor, thus four time series are simulated under
the damage case where all columns in the first floor (C1, C2 and C3) experience a sudden
reduction of stiffness, equal to 80% of reduction of the E-modulus. It is simulated that
the damage occurs at td = 50 s, such that the first 50 seconds contain the response in
the intact state, and the last 50 seconds represent the damaged state of the structure.
Figure 5.1 shows the horizontal acceleration responses obtained numerically at each floor,
which resembles what the sensor recordings would yield. These acceleration responses
are without noises.

5.1
Detection of Damage Time Instant
Most traditional vibration-based damage detection methods utilize the structural response
measurements before and after the occurrence of damage [45], hence they are not
applicable to detect damage on line and identify when the damage occurs. The advantage
of using the Hilbert-Huang transform for damage detecting is that it is a time-frequency
data processing tool, which can be utilized to acquire damage features to detect damage
time instants.
The first method to detect the damage time instant is the application of EMD to detect
spikes in the intrinsic mode functions. The other method is by applying the HHT and
tracking the frequency-time relation associated with maximum amplitude to detect changes
in the frequency over time, and thus detecting the damage time instant.
5.1.1
Damage spikes in intrinsic mode function
Deterioration of structure exposed to harsh environments may lead to a sudden change of
stiffness. This can include weld fracture, column buckling and brace breakage [2], which
in turn will cause a discontinuity in the acceleration response recorded in the proximity of
damage location at the damage time instant.
This basic concept was established by [46] using a wavelet-based approach to detect
damage time instant and damage locations on a simple structural model with breakage
springs. This was performed by decomposing vibration signals with the wavelet analysis
in the time domain. The discontinuity in the signals formed a damage spike in the wavelet
details so that the damage time instance could be identified. Yang et al. [2] adopted the
same idea but using the EMD to decompose the structural response signals to capture
the discontinuity. Xu et al. [28] present an experimental study of the same approach on a
3-story shear building model. Since the EMD is based on the local time scale of the signal,
it makes the approach favourable compared to the wavelet-based approach.
The approach in this project is to apply the EMD directly to the measured acceleration
time histories to decompose the data into IMFs. However, for detecting damage spikes,
an intermittency criterion is imposed during the sifting process. This ensures that the
resulting IMF extracted will only contain data with frequencies over the chosen intermittency
frequency, fc. When an appropriate intermittency frequency is imposed through the
EMD as a high-pass filter in each sifting, a sharp spike can be detected in the first IMF
extracted. Figure 5.2 shows the first IMF extracted for each sensor measurement where
fc = 475 Hz. From this, it can be seen that the damage occurs at 50 seconds in the
measured acceleration responses.
These spikes will not be detectable unless the intermittency frequency, fc, is smaller than
the frequency of the discontinuity. Still, the intermittency frequency should be larger than
the highest structural frequency appearing in the acceleration measurement. The MATLAB
script for this approach can be found in Appendix D.1.
5.1.2
Tracking of the time-frequency relation
The second method investigated for detecting damage time instants is by tracking the
frequency over time to detect when the frequency rapidly drops to a lower level, indicating
a sudden reduction of stiffness. Supposing only one acceleration response was recorded,
at the top floor in sensor 4, denoted as ¨x4(t). The procedure starts with obtaining the
Fourier spectrum of the acceleration response ¨x4(t), see Figure 5.3. It can be seen that
each of the 3 natural frequencies has been split into two spikes in the Fourier spectrum,
indicating the existence of damage. The first modal response is isolated to identify the

5.2
Identification of Natural Frequencies Before and After
Damage
By comparing dynamic properties of a system, such as natural frequencies, damping ratios
and mode shapes, damage detection is achieved on level 1, i.e. identification of damage
existence [47]. This section is dedicated to describing the procedures used to identify
the natural frequencies before and after damage. The first procedure is based on the
approach proposed by Yang et al. [2] where the frequency-time relation associated with
maximum amplitude is used. The second approach is similar to the one proposed by Lin
et al. [42], where the correlation functions of the responses are utilized as input for the
HHT to calculate the instantaneous phase, which in turn are used to calculate the natural
frequencies.
After determining the damage time instant, td = 50 s, the signal is split into two segments,
one from 0-30s as the intact signal and the other from 70-100s as the damaged signal, for
some tolerance. Only one measurement at one location is necessary for both methods to
identify the frequencies, e.g. the response measured at the top floor from sensor 4.
5.2.1
Frequency-time relation associated with maximum amplitude
To identify the natural frequencies before and after damage, the modal responses can
be decomposed with EMD and Hilbert transform into the Hilbert amplitude spectrum as
described in section 5.1.2. After the damage time instant is determined and the signal
is separated into two segments, the Fourier spectra are constructed, see Figure 5.7a
and 5.7b. It can be observed from the intact state Fourier spectrum that three natural
frequencies f1, f2 and f3 are within these ranges: 9 Hz < f1 < 11 Hz, 27 Hz < f2 < 31 Hz
and 48 Hz < f3 < 51 Hz. For the damaged state the following ranges are chosen:
5 Hz < f1 < 7.5 Hz, 21 Hz < f2 < 26 Hz and 42 Hz < f3 < 46 Hz.

5.2.2
Autocorrelation function and the Hilbert-Huang Transform
The second approach for identifying the natural frequencies before and after the damage
is based on the correlation function of the modal responses and the HHT to compute the
instantaneous phase. This approach is only applicable to linear structures with normal
modes exposed to ambient vibrations as it is similar to the approach proposed in [42].

Autocorrelation function
A random vibration signal is characterised by its lack of predictability. The value of a
random vibration at any point is unknown and cannot be predicted from knowledge of
values at previous time instants; hence it is said to have non-deterministic behaviour. The
characteristics of a random signal is that it can only be described in terms of averages [48].
The mean value for a discrete signal x(n) is defined as shown in Equation 5.1, and the
mean value is often referred to as the expectation of the random variable x(n), denoted
as E[x(n)].
µ =
1
N∆t
N
X
n=1
x(n)∆t = E[x(n)]
(5.1)
It is interesting to determine how adjacent points are correlated when considering a signal,
x(t). It is expected that the correlation is high for points close to each other, and as
the distance between the two points increases, the correlation is lower. Then, when the
points are far away, the correlation is practically zero. This correlation can be defined as
shown in Equation 5.2 and is referred to as the autocorrelation function. In practice, time
averaging is used to obtain the autocorrelation function for discrete time series, as shown
in Equation 5.3. A similar correlation can be found between two different signals, defined
as the cross-correlation function, see Equation 5.4 and 5.5.
Rx(τ) = E[x(t)x(t + τ)]
(5.2)
Rx(m) =
1

N−m

x(n)x(n + m)
(5.3)

(5.4)

(5.5)
Natural excitation technique
The natural excitation technique has been discussed extensively in [49] and [50], regarding
that ambient vibrations can be simulated as uncorrelated white noise excitations. Thus, if
the excitation is regarded as an ambient vibration, the cross-correlation function of two
responses will have the same properties as the structure’s impulse response function
according to Farrar and James [51]. Later in this section, it will be demonstrated that the
Damage Detection of Structures
39
==PAGE:56==
correlation functions can be expressed as a summation of decaying sinusoids, each with
a damped natural frequency and damping ratio identical to the ones corresponding to a
structural mode. In this way, the correlation function can be treated as though it was a
free decaying response, i.e. a sum of decaying sinusoids.
From the standard matrix form equation of motion:

==PAGE:57==
Proposed method for identified natural frequencies before and after damage
The approach in [42] is based on defining the cross-spectral density from the measured
acceleration responses and then obtain the cross-correlation function by inverse FFT.
Furthermore, the cross-correlation function is decomposed into modal components and
processed through the HHT to identify the natural frequencies. In this project, the modal
responses are first extracted from the measured acceleration using the EMD. The first
IMF obtained will be quite close to the rth modal response. Thereafter, the autocorrelation
functions are directly computed for the first IMFs; one for each modal response. As
demonstrated previously, the autocorrelation will have the same characteristics as the
impulse response function for the same structure. Lastly, the Hilbert spectral analysis can
then be used to obtain the instantaneous phase of the autocorrelation function.
To isolate the modal responses from the measured acceleration response, the EMD
together with band-pass filtering is utilized as proposed by Yang et al. [30]. This procedure
is previously described more in detail in section 5.2.1. With this approach, the modal
responses are easily obtained, and the noises outside the frequency range from the
band-pass filter are removed. Furthermore, the same band-passed signals as shown in
Figure 5.8 are also utilized for this approach. The autocorrelation functions are computed
directly on the first IMF extracted in each bandpass signal, see Figure 5.10, and will be
treated as the modal impulse response functions. The function dircor.m in MATLAB from
the OMAtools V1.5 provided by Rune Brincker and Carlos Ventura [52] has been utilized
to compute the correlation functions in this thesis.
As described in [30], the HHT can be used for identifying natural frequencies from impulse
acceleration responses of a linear structure with normal modes at p (p = 1, ..., n) DOF,
denoted as ¨xr
p(t). The impulse loading is applied at the kth DOF. The basic idea is that for
an impulse acceleration response, ¨xr
p(t), the Hilbert transform can be obtained so that the
analytical signal is given as:
Y r
p (t) = ¨xr
p(t) + i˜¨xr
p(t) = Ar
p(t)eiθr
p(t)
(5.10)
where
Y r
p (t) is the analytical signal
¨xr
p(t) is the impulse acceleration response for the rth modal response
˜¨xr
p(t) is the Hilbert transform of the impulse response
Ar
p(t) is the instantaneous amplitude for the rth modal response
θr
p(t) is the instantaneous phase for the rth modal response
It was also derived by Yang et al. [30], that for a case with small damping ratios, ζ < 10%,
and large natural frequencies, ωr
n, the phase angle can be given by
θr
p(t) = ωr
dt + ϕr + 3π
2 + ϕr
p,k
(5.11)
where
θr
p(t) is the instantaneous phase angle associated with the rth mode
ωr
d is the rth damped modal frequency
ϕr is the phase lag of the rth mode
ϕr
p,k is the phase difference between the pth element and the kth element in the rth mode
shape.
Damage Detection of Structures
41
==PAGE:58==
From Equation 5.11 is can be seen that the time differentiation of the phase is equal to the
rth damped modal frequency:
ωr(t) = dθr
p(t)/dt = ωr
d.
(5.12)
In practice, the modal frequency is obtained by plotting the phase angle θpr(t) versus time
and fit it to a straight line using a linear least-squares fit procedure. This line is referred to
as the average linear least-square line for the phase angle. Consequently, the slope of
that line is the modal frequency, ωr
d, see Figure 5.11.

6
Sensitivity Analysis of Method
This chapter is dedicated to the sensitivity analysis that was performed to evaluate the
robustness of the different implementations of the HHT for damage detection, described
in chapter 5. The different damage scenarios used for this analysis are firstly presented.
Moreover, the factors that will be investigated include; frequency content of the structures
analysed, damage severity, location of damage, duration of signals and presence of noise.
The last factor investigated is the choice of bandwidths.
6.1
Damage Scenarios
Different damage scenarios have been simulated for the three structures presented in
chapter 4 to be able to test the stability and sensitivity of the method. Eight cases in total
have been used for this purpose, see Table 6.1. Each structure has two similar cases of
damage, so that comparisons can be made. This includes case number 1, 3 and 7 with
80% stiffness reduction of the columns in the first floor, while case number 2, 4 and 8
represent a 30% reduction of stiffness of the same columns. In order to investigate the
influence of different damage locations, cases 5 and 6 have been simulated. This is done
only for structure B to minimize the number of damage scenarios since it has the most
realistic dimensions and properties.
Table 6.1: Damage scenarios
Structure A

The damage severity, i.e. the percentage of stiffness reduction in elements, has been
chosen so that it would result in frequency changes that could be found in structures
exposed to excessive loads. On the other hand, some of the cases included have a much
lower damage severity. The frequency changes for cases 2, 4 and 8 could therefore
represent smaller damage, not necessarily from an extreme event. Table 6.2 shows the
first three modal frequencies obtained from the FE-model for the intact states and for the
different damage cases.

6.2
Frequency Content of Structure
The EMD method has a deficiency in that it may induce undesirable IMF components in the
lower frequency regions [45]. Thus, the effect that the frequency content of a structure may
have on the results is investigated in this section. For this, the three structures presented
in chapter 4 are utilized by investigating the results obtained for cases 1, 3 and 7.
6.2.1
Structure A - Case 1
For this case, the damage detection scheme described in chapter 5 is investigated on the
measurements from sensor 3 (S3) only, except for detecting the damage spike where all
sensors are utilized.
Detection of damage time instant
The damage time instant, td = 50 s, is detected using both approaches presented in
chapter 5. The intermittency frequency used for detecting the damage spike is fc = 300 Hz.
In Figure 6.1, the damage spike can only be observed for the first sensor. This can also
give an indication according to [2] that the damage is also located near sensor 1, which
holds true for this case as the damaged elements are the two columns in the first floor.
The time-frequency relation associated with maximum amplitude is obtained for the first
modal component to track the frequency changes. From Figure 6.2 a clear drop in the
frequency can be observed around 50 s, indicating a damage occurring around that time
instant.
Identification of natural frequencies
The second step in the damage detection scheme in this work is to identify the modal
frequencies before and after damage. The first approach is to plot the time-frequency
relations for each mode, intact and damaged state, associated with maximum amplitude in
the Hilbert amplitude spectrum. To identify the natural frequencies, the mean value of each
frequency-time plot is determined, shown as dashed lines in Figure 6.3. The identified
natural frequencies for case 1 using this approach are listed in Table 6.3.
The second approach involves using the autocorrelation function, see Appendix E.1, of

6.2.4
Comments
From investigating the applicability of the method and the results obtained on three struc-
tures with very different frequency content, the method seems reliable and identifies the
damage in all three cases with reasonable accuracy. However, the results could indicate
that the method using the autocorrelation has a slightly improved accuracy in identifying
the natural frequencies for the higher modes in the lower frequency structures. On the
other hand, it should also be mentioned that the lower frequency structures will have larger
relative errors in percentage compared with the higher frequency structure for the same
deviance in identified frequencies. This aspect should thus also be taken into consideration
when comparing the results obtained from different structures.
Damage Detection of Structures
53
==PAGE:70==
6.3
Damage Severity
Another factor that can influence the results from the damage detection scheme is the
damage severity, thus is this factor also included in the sensitivity analysis. The results are
summarized in the tables with the identified frequencies together with the most important
graphs, and the rest of the figures can be found in Appendix E.2.
6.3.1
Structure A - Case 2
Sensor measurement from S3 at the top floor is again used for detecting damage, except
detection of damage spikes.
Detection of damage time instant
The damage scenario in case 2 involves a 30% stiffness reduction of the lowest two columns.
As seen in Figure 6.13, the damage spike is still detectable in sensor 1. Compared to case
1, ref. Figure 6.1, the amplitude of the damage spike is somewhat reduced, which can
be associated with the change in damage severity. On the other hand, the damage time
instant is not as clearly detectable tracking the frequency-time relation associated with
maximum amplitude for the second modal response, see Figure 6.14. But, a slight drop in
the frequency can still be observed around 50 seconds.

6.3.4
Comments
By investigating cases with smaller damage severity, the results show that the damage
spikes have lower amplitudes which makes it more difficult to detect the damage time
instant. The same can be observed for the method tracking the frequency over time, where
smaller damage, i.e. smaller frequency change, is harder to detect on the time-frequency
plots. The second mode has a larger change in frequency compared to the first mode for
cases 2, 4 and 8. This is the reason why the second modal response has been used to
detect the damage time instant.
On the other hand, the identification of natural frequencies does not seem to be affected by
the damage severity by comparing the results for cases 1 and 2, cases 3 and 4, and cases
7 and 8. Another observation is that the frequencies identified with the autocorrelation are,
in general more precise compared to the ones obtained from the frequency-time relations.
This observation is even more prominent in these cases with lower damage severity.

6.4
Damage Location
The next parameter to investigate is the damage location to see if the HHT is able to detect
the damages nonetheless and to compare the accuracy of the method for different damage
locations.
6.4.1
Structure B - Case 5
The damage in case 5 is simulated as an 80% stiffness reduction of the columns in the
top floor. Only sensor measurements from the top floor (S4) are utilized for detecting the
damage, except for identifying the damage time instant with spikes in the intrinsic mode
functions.

sensors 1 and 2, while no spike is detected for sensor 4, which is closest to the damaged
elements. The damage time instant can also be identified by tracking the frequency over
time to detect when the frequency drops to a lower level indicating damage, see Figure 6.20.
In this case, the frequency of the second modal response is used to track any changes
since it showed a larger shift in peak frequencies from the FFT-spectrum in Figure 6.21.

Comments
Comparing the results obtained for cases 5 and 6 with case 3, the method seems to detect
the damages accurately in terms of natural frequencies. This shows that the method
can identify the natural frequencies relatively precise regardless of where the damage is
located on the structure. However, the damage spikes are not representing the correct
sensor location for case 5, which makes the method not reliable for indicating damage
location. On the other hand, the damage time instant was attainable with both methods
regardless of the damage location.
It is again observed that the method using the autocorrelation performed better at identifying
the higher modal frequencies compared to the method using the frequency-time relation.

6.5
Duration of Signals
A common issue for most signal processing methods is dealing with too short data signals.
This effect is therefore studied to examine if the accuracy of the HHT for identifying natural
frequencies is affected by the duration of signals used. As stated in [31] the EMD and
Hilbert spectral analysis can accurately process short time series. Thus, it is expected that
the method is not severely affected by the signal duration.
All three structures have been investigated. However, only the results for structure B are
represented in the text. Results for structures A (case 2) and C (case 8) can be found in
Appendix E.4.
6.5.1
Structure B - Case 4
Three different signal lengths are investigated; 100 s, 200 s, and 340 s. The damage occur
at td = 50 s, td = 100 s, and td = 170 s respectively, and thus the signal duration used for
identifying natural frequencies after the signal is divided are 30 s, 80 s and 150 s. After
the damage time instant is detected, the signals are split into two signals; one intact and
one damaged, with 20 seconds tolerance to each side from the damage time instant.
Detection of damage time instant
The damage time instant was detectable for all signal duration investigated. Results can
be found in Appendix E.4.
Identification of natural frequencies
The frequencies identified for case 4 with a signal of 100 s can be found in Table 6.11 and
Table 6.12. To examine the effect of signal duration, the identified natural frequencies using
200 s and 340 s signals are listed in Table 6.19, 6.20, and Table 6.21, 6.22 respectively.

6.6
Noise Contamination
A critical factor to investigate is the noise contamination and how different levels of noise
present in the signal will affect the accuracy of the method. In signals measured from real
structures, noise will inevitably be present to some extent. Since all signals investigated in
this project are simulated, Gaussian white noise is added to the signals to resemble noise
present in real-life signals. The contaminated signals are simulated by adding zero-mean
white noise with unit standard deviation to the original signal as
xnoise(t) = x(t) + RMS(x(t)) · Nlevel · w(t)
(6.1)
where xnoise(t) denotes the signal with noise, x(t) is the signal without noise, RMS(x(t))
is the root-mean-square of x(t), Nlevel is the noise level, and w(t) Gaussian white noise
vector with zero-mean and unit standard deviation. Figure 6.24 illustrated the added noise
to a signal from structure B.
