Figure 6.24: Signal with added noise level Nlevel = 10%
Noise levels of 10%, 20%, and 50% are utilized for investigating the robustness of the
method against noise. All cases are examined for robustness against noise, however only
cases 3 and 7 are presented in the text. The results from the remaining cases can be
found in Appendix E.5.

see Figure 6.25.

6.6.3
Comments
By investigating the robustness against noise contamination of signals, the method for
detecting the damage time instant through spikes in the IMFs appears to perform poorly
in the cases with noise present. This is because the amplitudes of the spikes with no
noise present are smaller than the amplitude of the added noise. Thus, according to [2]
the damage spikes will be merged in the noise, and the EMD will not be able to detect
the spikes. On the other hand, the second method of detecting the damage time instant
through tracking the frequency-time relation is demonstrated to be quite robust against
noise contamination. The damage time instant is properly obtainable even with a noise
level of 50%.
From the investigations of noise contamination performed on all damage cases, both
methods presented to identify the natural frequencies before and after damage appear
to be quite robust against noise. The frequency-time relation method seems to be more
stable for the lower frequency structures compared to structure C when noise is present.
Moreover, the auto-correlation method generally shows better robustness against noise
contamination than results obtained from the average of the frequency-time relation. This
can be related to the fact that the autocorrelation function of white noise is the Dirac
delta function at T = 0 and zero when T ̸= 0 due to the independence of white noise at
different time instants. If it is assumed that the dynamic response, x(t), and the white
noise, w(t), are independent of each other, the autocorrelation function can be written
as: R(τ) = E[x(t)x(t + τ)] + σ2δ(τ), where σ2 is the variance of the noise and δ(τ) is the
Dirac delta function.
Damage Detection of Structures
67
==PAGE:84==
6.7
Bandwidths
The choice of bandwidths for defining the frequency ranges of each modal response is a
subjective task. Thus, this factor is shortly investigated in the four cases for structure B to
see how it affects the results of the identified frequencies.
6.7.1
Identification of natural frequencies
In this section, only the methods for identifying the natural frequencies are examined. The
cases for structure B (case 3, 4, 5 and 6) are investigated, but only case 3 will be presented
in the text. The results from the other cases can be found in Appendix E.6.
Structure B - Case 3
The bandwidths used to identify the natural frequencies listed in Table 6.27 and Table 6.29,
denoted as ’BW1’ are;

Comments
There is a trend showing that the narrower bandwidths chosen tend to yield slightly more
accurate results. However, the overall results do not deviate much by changing the
bandwidths, so it can indicate that the EMD and HT are not vastly affected by using a
wider bandwidth for defining the modal responses.

6.8
Additional investigations
Some additional investigations were performed for improving and further reviewing the
application of HHT for the proposed damage detection scheme in this project. This includes
increasing the sampling frequency to detect damage spikes in structural responses with
noise present and to check how the randomness in the load can affect the variability of the
identified natural frequencies.
As it was described in section 6.6, the method for determining the damage time instant
by spikes in the IMFs was not possible for cases with noise present. However, if a lower
noise level is present, for instance 5%, the spikes can be obtained. Figure 6.27 illustrates
that a damage spike can be obtained for case 7 when 5% noise is present in the signal.

For structure B, on the other hand, the damage spike could still not be obtained when
the noise level was lowered to 5%. Thus, it was investigated to see if the spike could be
obtained by increasing the sampling frequency from fs = 1000 Hz to fs = 2000 Hz. It was
observed that the damage spike could be obtained for case 3 with 5% noise when the
sampling frequency was higher, see Figure 6.28. The same observation was also made
by [28], concluding that the sampling frequency should be higher than the frequency of
the damage event for successful application of the EMD for detecting the damage time
instants.

This shows that the method for identifying the natural frequencies has a variation of 2-3%
from the frequency obtained from the modal analysis. It is also known that different modal

seem to have reasonable accuracy and performance against the randomness of input
loads.
72
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:89==
7
Conclusion and Further Work
7.1
Discussion of Results
The main objective of this project was to investigate a new method for detecting damage in
structures that only uses measured acceleration responses from sensors mounted on the
structure. The method of the Hilbert-Huang transform was initially chosen as it is applicable
to nonlinear signals since most real-life signals are, in fact, nonlinear or non-stationary.
After reviewing the literature, four approaches were chosen to follow for performing damage
detection on level 1 for three different 2D-frame structures with linear responses. The first
two cover the detection of damage time instant through obtaining damage spikes in the
IMFs and by tracking the frequency-time relations of the modal components. The last two
methods are used to identify the natural frequencies before and after damage. A sensitivity
analysis of the four approaches was conducted to evaluate the overall performance and
robustness of the four applications of the HHT for level 1 damage detection.
The method of detecting damage spikes for identifying the damage time instant is shown
to only perform well with no noise present in the signals. Even though the results could be
improved by increasing the sampling frequency, the method shows to have unfavorable
attributes. The detection of damage spikes is also only possible for cases with sudden
damage. On the other hand, the second approach for detecting the damage time instant
by tracking changes in the frequency over time appears to be robust against noise con-
tamination and other factors that can affect the method’s performance. Even if the method
can have trouble detecting the damage time instant for damage of smaller severity, it was
still possible to do so in most cases. In these cases, the Fourier spectrum can indicate
which mode is mostly affected by the damage, in terms of frequency change, to facilitate
the detection of damage time instant. Another advantage of using the second method is
that it can detect damage reflected by a gradual change in stiffness in the time domain.
Although this type of damage has not been investigated, the theory and literature reviewed
in the report support this claim.
For identifying the natural frequencies, two methods were investigated. The first one
uses the frequency-time relation associated with maximum amplitude, and the other uses
the instantaneous phase from the autocorrelation function calculated for the structural
responses. Both methods appear to give results with reasonable accuracy for the various
damage cases investigated. In some cases, for instance, with noise and lower damage
severity, the method using the autocorrelation function tended to produce slightly more
accurate results than the other method. However, this method is restricted to only being
applicable on linear signals with ambient input excitations, making the other method more
favourable due to its possibility for a wider range of applications. This advantage outweighs
the slightly improved accuracy of the identified natural frequencies from the other method.
7.2
Conclusion
When sudden damage occurs, the measured acceleration response data can be used to
detect damage during that period. The approaches investigated in this project shows to
accomplish level 1 damage detection to a quite satisfactory degree.
Damage Detection of Structures
73
==PAGE:90==
The main concluding remarks are as follows.
• The damage time instants are detected by identifying damage spikes in the recorded
data by processing it through the EMD with intermittency criteria. However, this
approach relies on the damage spikes, which are statistical variables depending
on the damage severity, noise contamination and other factors. Therefore, as it
is shown in the sensitivity analysis, the approach with damage spikes does not
produce satisfactory results if the damage is small or the level of noise pollution is
high. Nevertheless, from examining the results from this approach, the simulation
data indicate that the damage spike always can be obtained if the measured data is
free of noise or the level of noise contamination is very small.
• By using the HHT for tracking the modal frequency in the time domain, the damage
time instant could also be identified as a sudden frequency drop. This approach also
has the advantage of only relying on one sensor measurement at one location on
the structure. Furthermore, as seen from the sensitivity analysis, the method can
accurately identify the damage time instant regardless of the noise level.
• The method of computing the average of the frequency-time relations with HHT for
identifying the natural frequencies before and after the damage, is shown to perform
with substantial precision even with high levels of noise.
• Lastly, the second approach for identifying the natural frequencies was using the
autocorrelation functions as input for the HHT to obtain the instantaneous phase
angles. This approach is demonstrated to be applicable on linear structures exposed
to ambient excitations only. Moreover, the method’s precision is slightly better than
the other approach for identifying natural frequencies. Due to the limited application
of the second approach, the first method is evaluated to be more favourable for
damage detection, despite the slightly lower accuracy.
7.3
Further Work
The HHT was shown to be a promising tool for damage detection, as anticipated from
the literature review. However, due to the project’s time limit, some further investigations
could be conducted to improve or widen the application of the methods applied in this
thesis. Furthermore, the methods in this project are only applied to simulated signals from
a numerical model, with artificial damage imposed on the structure. Therefore, the method
should also be investigated further on signals measured from real structures for further
work.
The last three weeks of the project was dedicated to investigating a possible damage
localization scheme based on the Hilbert marginal spectrum. However, some issues with
the implementation of the scheme were still unresolved, and further research should be
carried out to fulfil the damage detection on level 2. It should, in principle, be possible to
detect the damage by defining a damage index for each sensor location to identify the
sensor closest to the damage. From the literature review on the use of HHT for damage
localization, the damage indices based on energy features seem to be the preferred
choice. Finding the best damage localization scheme based on the HHT and evaluating
its robustness could be a thesis in itself.
Initially, the main idea behind this project was to find a new method for detecting damage
in structures with nonlinear responses. Much time was dedicated to exploring the different
aspects of the methodology and then applying it to some simple linear cases to secure
the correct implementation. Due to the time limitation of the project, the method was
74
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:91==
not investigated on nonlinear signals. This could be done by simulating responses from
several strong ground motions known to push the structure into the nonlinear regime and
investigating the method’s performance to detect damage by utilizing those responses.
Lastly, the EMD is also greatly discussed with its advantages and disadvantages. However,
for cases with closely spaced modes, the classical EMD approach can face difficulties
separating the modes. Furthermore, this are a common issue in signals from civil structures.
In these cases, if the approach followed in this thesis cannot be used to separate the
modes, the ensemble empirical mode decomposition (EEMD) is recommended to used
instead. This EEMD has been preferred over the classical EMD mostly for its ability to
treat mode-mixing and since it is even more robust against noise contamination. From
this, it is strongly recommended to investigate the use of EEMD for decomposing both
nonlinear signals and signals with closely spaced modes.

% EMD:
Emprical mode decomposition
3
%
4
% imf = emd_mit(x)
5
%
6
% x
- input signal (must be a column vector)
7
%
8
% This version will calculate all the imf's (longer)
9
%
10
% imf - Matrix of intrinsic mode functions (each as a row)
11
%
with residual in last row.
12
%
13
% See:
Huang et al, Royal Society Proceedings on Math, Physical ,
14
%
and Engineering Sciences , vol. 454, no. 1971, pp. 903-995,
15
%
8 March 1998
16
%
17
% Author: Ivan Magrin -Chagnolleau
<ivan@ieee.org>
18
%
19
20
function imf = emd_mit(x);
21
22
c = x(:)'; % copy of the input signal (as a row vector)
23
N = length(x);
24
25
%-------------------------------------------------------------------------
26
% loop to decompose the input signal into successive IMF
27
28
imf = []; % Matrix which will contain the successive IMF, and the residue
29
30
while (1) % the stop criterion is tested at the end of the loop
31
32
%-------------------------------------------------------------------------
33
% inner loop to find each imf
34
35
h = c; % at the beginning of the sifting process , h is the signal
36
SD = 1; % Standard deviation which will be used to stop the sifting process
37
38
while SD > 0.3
39
% while the standard deviation is higher than 0.3 (typical value)
40
41
% find local max/min points
42
d = diff(h); % approximate derivative
43
maxmin = []; % to store the optima (min and max without distinction so
far)
44
for i=1:N-2
45
if d(i)==0
% we are on a zero
46
maxmin = [maxmin, i];
47
elseif sign(d(i))~=sign(d(i+1))
% we are straddling a zero so
48
maxmin = [maxmin, i+1];
% define zero as at i+1 (not i)
49
end
50
end
51
52
if size(maxmin ,2) < 2 % then it is the residue
53
break
54
end
82
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:99==
55
56
% divide maxmin into maxes and mins
57
if maxmin(1)>maxmin(2)
% first one is a max not a min
58
maxes = maxmin(1:2:length(maxmin));
59
mins
= maxmin(2:2:length(maxmin));
60
else
% is the other way around
61
maxes = maxmin(2:2:length(maxmin));
62
mins
= maxmin(1:2:length(maxmin));
63
end
64
65
% make endpoints both maxes and mins
66
maxes = [1 maxes N];
67
mins
= [1 mins
N];
68
69
70
%
-------------------------------------------------------------------------
71
% spline interpolate to get max and min envelopes; form imf
72
maxenv = spline(maxes,h(maxes),1:N);
73
minenv = spline(mins, h(mins),1:N);
74
75
m = (maxenv + minenv)/2; % mean of max and min enveloppes
76
prevh = h; % copy of the previous value of h before modifying it
77
h = h - m; % substract mean to h
78
79
% calculate standard deviation
80
eps = 0.0000001; % to avoid zero values
81
SD = (norm(prevh-h,2)/norm(prevh ,2))^2;
82
83
end
84
85
imf = [imf; h]; % store the extracted IMF in the matrix imf
86
% if size(maxmin ,2)<2, then h is the residue
87
88
% stop criterion of the algo.
89
if size(maxmin ,2) < 2
90
break
91
end
92
93
c = c - h; % substract the extracted IMF from the signal
94
95
end
96
97
return

%NHT

% EMD:
Emprical mode decomposition with intermittency criteria
67
%
68
% imf = emd_inter(t,x,int,fs)
69
%
70
% t
- time vector
71
% x
- input signal (must be a column vector)
72
% int - intermittency frequency [Hz]
73
% fs
- sampling frequency [Hz]
74
%
75
% This version will calculate all the imf's (longer)
76
%
77
% imf - Matrix of intrinsic mode functions (each as a row)
78
%
with residual in last row.
79
%
80
% See:
Huang et al, Royal Society Proceedings on Math, Physical ,
81
%
and Engineering Sciences , vol. 454, no. 1971, pp. 903-995,
82
%
8 March 1998
83
%
84
% Author: Ivan Magrin -Chagnolleau
<ivan@ieee.org>
85
% Modified by: Doris Popovic <s192766@student.dtu.dk>
86
87
function imf = emd_inter(t,x,int,fs);
88
89
c = x; % copy of the input signal
90
N = length(x);
91
92
%-------------------------------------------------------------------------
93
% loop to decompose the input signal into successive IMF
94
95
imf = []; % Matrix which will contain the successive IMF, and the residue
96
97
while (1) % the stop criterion is tested at the end of the loop
98
99
%-------------------------------------------------------------------------
100
% inner loop to find each imf
101
102
h = c; % at the beginning of the sifting process , h is the signal
103
SD = 1; % Standard deviation which will be used to stop the sifting process
104
105
iterCount = 0;
106
107
while SD > 0.3 && iterCount <= 10
108
% while the standard deviation is higher than 0.3 (typical value)
109
110
h = highpass(h,int,fs);
111
112
% spline interpolate to get max and min envelopes; form imf
113
[indmin, indmax] = extr(h,t);
% find extrema
114
nbsym = 2;
% number of points added to each end
115
98
Damage Detection of Structures
==PAGE:115==
116
% stop criterion of the algo.
117
if length(indmin) + length(indmax) < 2
118
break
119
end
120
121
if length(indmin) + length(indmax) >= 4
122
[tmin,tmax,zmin,zmax] = boundary_conditions_emd(indmin ,indmax ,t,h,h,
nbsym);
% ASSE
123
else
124
% make endpoints both maxes and mins
125
indmin
= [1 indmin N];
126
indmax
= [1 indmax
N];
127
tmin = t(indmin);
128
tmax = t(indmax);
129
zmin = h(indmin);
130
zmax = h(indmax);
131
end
132
133
maxenv(1,:) = interp1(tmin,zmin,t,'spline'); % max envelope
134
minenv(1,:) = interp1(tmax,zmax,t,'spline'); % min envelope
135
136
m = (maxenv + minenv)/2; % mean of max and min enveloppes
137
prevh = h; % copy of the previous value of h before modifying it
138
h = h - m; % substract mean to h
139
140
% calculate standard deviation
141
eps = 0.0000001; % to avoid zero values
142
143
%SD = (norm(prevh-h,2)/norm(prevh ,2))^2;
144
SD = sum ( ((prevh - h).^2) ./ (prevh.^2 + eps) );
145
146
iterCount = iterCount + 1;
147
end
148
149
150
imf = [imf; h]; % store the extracted IMF in the matrix imf
151
% if size(maxmin ,2)<2, then h is the residue
152
153
if length(imf(:,1))>1
154
break
155
end
156
157
% stop criterion of the algo.
158
if length(indmin) + length(indmax) < 2
159
break
160
end
161
162
c = c - h; % substract the extracted IMF from the signal
163
164
end
165
166
return

D.4
Theoretical derivation of cross-correlation function of
structural response with white noise excitation
From the standard matrix form equation of motion:
[M0]{¨x(t)} + [C0]{ ˙x(t)} + [K0]{x(t)} = {f(t)},
(D.1)
the modal coordinates can be expressed as
{x(t)} = [Φ]{q(t)} =
n
X
r=1
{φr}qr(t)
{ ˙x(t)} = [Φ]{ ˙q(t)} =
n
X
r=1
{φr} ˙qr(t)
{¨x(t)} = [Φ]{¨q(t)} =
n
X
r=1
{φr}¨qr(t)
(D.2)
where
[Φ] is the modal matrix
{q(t)} is a vector of modal coordinates
{φr} is the rth mode shape.
By combining Equation D.1 and Equation D.2 while using the orthogonal properties of
mode shapes, the equation of motion in D.1 can be decoupled into n modes
{¨qr(t)} + 2ζrωr
n{ ˙qr(t)} + ωr2
n {qr(t)} = {φr}T {f(t)}/mr
(D.3)
where
ωr
n is the rth modal frequency
ζr is the rth modal damping ratio
mr is the rth modal mass.
Solving Equation D.3 with the Duhamel integral assuming a general {f} and zero initial
conditions, the following expression is obtained
qr(t) =
Z t
−∞
{φr}t {f(τ)}gr(t −τ)dτ
(D.4)
where
gr(t) =
1
mrωr

exp (−ζrωr

dt)
ωr

Equation D.2 and D.4 can then be used to find a solution for {x(t)}:
{x(t)} =
n
X

{φr} ·
Z t
−∞
{φr}t {f(τ)}gr(t −τ)dτ
(D.5)
For a single output, xik(t), due to a single input force at point k, fk(t), Equation D.5 can
be written as

The summations on s and k are constants multiplied with the sine function with constant

This shows that the cross-correlation function of a structural response output with a white-
noise input is a sum of decaying sinusoids on the same form as the impulse response
function of the original system. This similarity allows the use of the correlation function for
time-domain modal parameter identification, that is based on using the impulse response
function.
